# 276\n
{Cambridge, Gonville and Caius 234/120, pp. 1-185: extracts from Ancrene Riwle}\n
C13b1\n
378 275 N\n
{=NW Worcs=}\n
{=Sample tagged: pp. 1-59=}\n
{Script - an idiosyncratic mixed book hand with cursive features\n
including some suggesting Anglicana e.g. long <r> and 8-shaped <g>=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Thorn and wynn are not distinguished in this hand, both lacking\n
ascenders. Following my usual practice, I have however transcribed\n
them as separate letters according to context; otherwise reading\n
becomes very confused. In the first few pages W and V are used for [w]\n
alongside wynn. But wynn is mostly regularised (with a few exceptions)\n
after p. 4. There is also some difficulty in separating the usage of CC,\n
CT and TT because the scribe tends to use the same ligature for all\n
three, though there are no examples of CC in the part so far\n
tagged. I have not normalised as Wilson (1954) does in his EETS edition, but\n
have cT where the ligature is clearly made for TT. The use of lower\n
case c is so as to distinguish this purely orthographic usage from\n
genuine CT for [kt] and CT for [xt] in hands where OE -ht words have CT\n
as a variant. In these cases I transcribe CT. Cf. also Layamon A, hand\n
B who uses a similar ligature for all three uses as well as in [xt]\n
contexts. Some parts of p. 1 are difficult to read on film because of\n
discolouration. For these parts I have had to rely on the EETS edition\n
which seems extremely accurate judging by the text as a whole=}\n
{~p1~}\n
{>(\'*CONTra *IRAM\'(>}\n
{=ins, to the right hand part of the first line of text=}\n

$/P11N_ICH\n
$have/vps11_BABBE\n
{=sic - Scribes normally form \'h\' and \'b\' similarly with two strokes, an\n
ascender followed by a lobe drawn from some point on the ascender and\n
curving out and down. In the case of \'b\' it ideally meets the foot of\n
the ascender at the baseline. In the case of \'h\' it ideally meets the\n
baseline to the right of the ascender, sometimes descending below it and\n
often curving further inwards. Scribal mistaking of <h> and <b> where\n
intended \'h\' has a short limb and/or where it cuts in too much and hits\n
the ascender (forming a <b>-like lobe) is very common indeed.\n
Conversely, where a \'b\' is formed somewhat loosely and the lobe does not\n
properly meet the ascender it can easily be read as \'h\'. One then has to\n
judge whether the figura should be read as the littera that it\n
superficially looks like or as what was \'intended\'. The G scribe himself\n
differentiates these letters clearly; indeed he uses a different stroke\n
patterns to form them  \'h\' he forms traditionally with two strokes; \'b\'\n
he forms in a single stroke curving the lobe up from the bottom of the\n
ascender. It is often open at the top, failing to meet the ascender\n
further up.  The G scribe\'s miswriting of \'b\' for \'h\' was presumably\n
triggered by a \'b\'-like \'h\' in his exemplar, the error unnoticed because\n
the G scribe was writing at speed=}\n
$be/vSpp_I+BEO+N $ge-/xp-vpp_I+ $/vSpp[V]_+N\n

near the top of D and finishing at the level of the top of the lobe.\n
(From p. 46 he occasionally uses an S-shaped stroke to the\n
ascender instead of his usual comma-shaped stroke. The S-shape becomes\n
more frequent near the end of the manuscript.) Here, the hairline\n
stroke from the right of the ascender, and cutting across it to be\n
visible fractionally to the left, was almost certainly made before\n
the back of the D was drawn down. The first C-shaped stroke of the D\n
is made, the scribe lifts or lightens the pen somewhat and takes it up to\n
the top ready to make the down stroke to finish the letter. This\n
looping movement of the pen is usually made in the air but is not always\n
completely off the parchment as it is sometimes visible as a hairline\n
\'pot hook\' to the right of the main down stroke of the D. The scribe seems\n
sometimes not to bring the down stroke directly down from the circular\n
movement but to reposition the pen first, leaving a hairline trace near\n
the top of the D=}\n
{.}\n
